CentralNotice -->\n   </div>\n   <div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n   </div>\n   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">\n    Superconductivity\n   </h1>\n   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">\n     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia\n    </div>\n    <div id="contentSub">\n    </div>\n    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">\n     Jump to:\n     <a href="#mw-head">\n      navigation\n     </a>\n     ,\n     <a href="#p-search">\n      search\n     </a>\n    </div>\n    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">\n     <div class="mw-parser-output">\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meissner_effect_p1390048.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1716" data-file-width="2400" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Meissner_effect_p1390048.jpg/220px-Meissner_effect_p1390048.jpg" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Meissner_effect_p1390048.jpg/440px-Meissner_effect_p1390048.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meissner_effect_p1390048.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         A\n         <a href="/wiki/Magnet" title="Magnet">\n          magnet\n         </a>\n         levitating above a\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-temperature_superconductor" title="High-temperature superconductor">\n          high-temperature superconductor\n         </a>\n         , cooled with\n         <a href="/wiki/Liquid_nitrogen" title="Liquid nitrogen">\n          liquid nitrogen\n         </a>\n         . Persistent electric current flows on the surface of the superconductor, acting to exclude the magnetic field of the magnet (\n         <a href="/wiki/Faraday%27s_law_of_induction" title="Faraday\'s law of induction">\n          Faraday\'s law of induction\n         </a>\n         ). This current effectively forms an electromagnet that repels the magnet.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" (metallic)\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stickstoff_gek%C3%BChlter_Supraleiter_schwebt_%C3%BCber_Dauermagneten_2009-06-21.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2250" data-file-width="3375" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Stickstoff_gek%C3%BChlter_Supraleiter_schwebt_%C3%BCber_Dauermagneten_2009-06-21.jpg/220px-Stickstoff_gek%C3%BChlter_Supraleiter_schwebt_%C3%BCber_Dauermagneten_2009-06-21.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Stickstoff_gek%C3%BChlter_Supraleiter_schwebt_%C3%BCber_Dauermagneten_2009-06-21.jpg/330px-Stickstoff_gek%C3%BChlter_Supraleiter_schwebt_%C3%BCber_Dauermagneten_2009-06-21.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Stickstoff_gek%C3%BChlter_Supraleiter_schwebt_%C3%BCber_Dauermagneten_2009-06-21.jpg/440px-Stickstoff_gek%C3%BChlter_Supraleiter_schwebt_%C3%BCber_Dauermagneten_2009-06-21.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stickstoff_gek%C3%BChlter_Supraleiter_schwebt_%C3%BCber_Dauermagneten_2009-06-21.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         A high-temperature superconductor levitating above a magnet\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       <b>\n        Superconductivity\n       </b>\n       is a phenomenon of exactly zero\n       <a href="/wiki/Electrical_resistance_and_conductance" title="Electrical resistance and conductance">\n        electrical resistance\n       </a>\n       and expulsion of\n       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">\n        magnetic flux fields\n       </a>\n       occurring in certain materials, called\n       <b>\n        superconductors\n       </b>\n       , when\n       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cooling" title="wikt:cooling">\n        cooled\n       </a>\n       below a characteristic\n       <a href="/wiki/Phase_transition" title="Phase transition">\n        critical temperature\n       </a>\n       . It was discovered by Dutch physicist\n       <a href="/wiki/Heike_Kamerlingh_Onnes" title="Heike Kamerlingh Onnes">\n        Heike Kamerlingh Onnes\n       </a>\n       on April\xc2\xa08, 1911, in\n       <a href="/wiki/Leiden" title="Leiden">\n        Leiden\n       </a>\n       . Like\n       <a href="/wiki/Ferromagnetism" title="Ferromagnetism">\n        ferromagnetism\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_spectral_line" title="Atomic spectral line">\n        atomic spectral lines\n       </a>\n       , superconductivity is a\n       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">\n        quantum mechanical\n       </a>\n       phenomenon. It is characterized by the\n       <a href="/wiki/Meissner_effect" title="Meissner effect">\n        Meissner effect\n       </a>\n       , the complete ejection of\n       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">\n        magnetic field lines\n       </a>\n       from the interior of the superconductor as it transitions into the superconducting state. The occurrence of the Meissner effect indicates that superconductivity cannot be understood simply as the\n       <a href="/wiki/Idealization_(science_philosophy)" title="Idealization (science philosophy)">\n        idealization\n       </a>\n       of\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Perfect_conductor" title="Perfect conductor">\n         perfect conductivity\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       in\n       <a href="/wiki/Classical_physics" title="Classical physics">\n        classical physics\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The electrical resistance of a metallic\n       <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">\n        conductor\n       </a>\n       decreases gradually as temperature is lowered. In ordinary conductors, such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">\n        copper\n       </a>\n       or\n       <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">\n        silver\n       </a>\n       , this decrease is limited by impurities and other defects. Even near\n       <a href="/wiki/Absolute_zero" title="Absolute zero">\n        absolute zero\n       </a>\n       , a real sample of a normal conductor shows some resistance. In a superconductor; the resistance drops abruptly to zero when the material is cooled below its critical temperature. An\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">\n        electric current\n       </a>\n       through a loop of\n       <a href="/wiki/Superconducting_wire" title="Superconducting wire">\n        superconducting wire\n       </a>\n       can persist indefinitely with no power source.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-1">\n         [1]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daintith_2-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Daintith-2">\n         [2]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gallop_3-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Gallop-3">\n         [3]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Durrant_4-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Durrant-4">\n         [4]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In 1986, it was discovered that some\n       <a href="/wiki/Cuprate_superconductor" title="Cuprate superconductor">\n        cuprate\n       </a>\n       -\n       <a href="/wiki/Perovskite_(structure)" title="Perovskite (structure)">\n        perovskite\n       </a>\n       <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">\n        ceramic\n       </a>\n       materials have a critical temperature above 90\xc2\xa0K (\xe2\x88\x92183\xc2\xa0\xc2\xb0C).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bednorz_5-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Bednorz-5">\n         [5]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Such a high transition temperature is theoretically impossible for a\n       <a href="/wiki/Conventional_superconductor" title="Conventional superconductor">\n        conventional superconductor\n       </a>\n       , leading the materials to be termed\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-temperature_superconductors" title="High-temperature superconductors">\n        high-temperature superconductors\n       </a>\n       . The cheaply-available coolant\n       <a href="/wiki/Liquid_nitrogen" title="Liquid nitrogen">\n        liquid nitrogen\n       </a>\n       boils at 77\xc2\xa0K, and thus superconduction at higher temperatures than this facilitates many experiments and applications that are less practical at lower temperatures.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n      </p>\n      <div class="toc" id="toc">\n       <div class="toctitle">\n        <h2>\n         Contents\n        </h2>\n       </div>\n       <ul>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1">\n         <a href="#Classification">\n          <span Meissner effect\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10">\n           <a href="#London_moment">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             2.4\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             London moment\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n         </ul>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11">\n         <a href="#History_of_superconductivity">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           3\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           History of superconductivity\n          </span>\n         </a>\n         <ul>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12">\n           <a href="#London_theory">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             3.1\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             London theory\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13">\n           <a href="#Conventional_theories_.281950s.29">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             3.2\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Conventional theories (1950s)\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14">\n           <a href="#Further_history">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             3.3\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Further history\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n         </ul>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15">\n         <a href="#High-temperature_superconductivity">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           4\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           High-temperature superconductivity\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16">\n         <a href="#Applications">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           5\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Applications\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17">\n         <a href="#Nobel_Prizes_for_superconductivity">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           6\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Nobel Prizes for superconductivity\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18">\n         <a href="#See_also">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           7\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           See also\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19">\n         <a href="#References">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           8\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           References\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20">\n         <a href="#Further_reading">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           9\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Further reading\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21">\n         <a href="#External_links">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           10\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           External links\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n       </ul>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">\n        Classification\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Superconductivity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classification">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Superconductor_classification" title="Superconductor classification">\n        Superconductor classification\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       There are many criteria by which superconductors are classified. The most common are:\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Response_to_a_magnetic_field">\n        Response to a magnetic field\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Superconductivity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Response to a magnetic field">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       A superconductor can be\n       <i>\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_I_superconductor" title="Type I superconductor">\n         Type\xc2\xa0I\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       , meaning it has a single\n       <a href="/wiki/Critical_field" title="Critical field">\n        critical field\n       </a>\n       , above which all superconductivity is lost and below which the magnetic field is completely expelled from the superconductor; or\n       <i>\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_II_superconductor" title="Type II superconductor">\n         Type\xc2\xa0II\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       , meaning it has two critical fields, between which it allows partial penetration of the magnetic field through isolated points. These points are called\n       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_vortex" title="Quantum vortex">\n        vortices\n       </a>\n       . Furthermore, in multicomponent superconductors it is possible to have combination of the two behaviours. In that case the superconductor is of\n       <a href="/wiki/Type-1.5_superconductor" title="Type-1.5 superconductor">\n        Type-1.5\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="By_theory_of_operation">\n        By theory of operation\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Superconductivity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: By theory of operation">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       It is\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Conventional_superconductor" title="Conventional superconductor">\n         conventional\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       if it can be explained by the\n       <a href="/wiki/BCS_theory" title="BCS theory">\n        BCS theory\n       </a>\n       or its derivatives, or\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Unconventional_superconductor" title="Unconventional superconductor">\n         unconventional\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       , otherwise.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="By_critical_temperature">\n        By critical temperature\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Superconductivity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: By critical temperature">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       A superconductor is generally considered\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/High-temperature_superconductivity" title="High-temperature superconductivity">\n         high-temperature\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       if it reaches a superconducting state when cooled using\n       <a href="/wiki/Liquid_nitrogen" title="Liquid nitrogen">\n        liquid nitrogen\n       </a>\n       \xe2\x80\x93 that is, at only\n       <i>\n        T\n        <sub>\n         c\n        </sub>\n       </i>\n       &gt; 77\xc2\xa0K) \xe2\x80\x93 or\n       <i>\n        low-temperature\n       </i>\n       if more aggressive cooling techniques are required to reach its critical temperature.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="By_material">\n        By material\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Superconductivity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: By material">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       Superconductor material classes include\n       <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">\n        chemical elements\n       </a>\n       (e.g.\n       <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">\n        mercury\n       </a>\n       or\n       <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">\n        lead\n       </a>\n       ),\n       <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">\n        alloys\n       </a>\n       (such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Niobium-titanium" title="Niobium-titanium">\n        niobium-titanium\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanium-niobium" title="Germanium-niobium">\n        germanium-niobium\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Niobium_nitride" title="Niobium nitride">\n        niobium nitride\n       </a>\n       ),\n       <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">\n        ceramics\n       </a>\n       (\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/YBCO" title="YBCO">\n        YBCO\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_diboride" title="Magnesium diboride">\n        magnesium diboride\n       </a>\n       ),\n       <a href="/wiki/Iron-based_superconductor" title="Iron-based superconductor">\n        superconducting pnictides\n       </a>\n       (like fluorine-doped LaOFeAs) or\n       <a href="/wiki/Organic_superconductor" title="Organic superconductor">\n        organic superconductors\n       </a>\n       (\n       <a href="/wiki/Fullerene" title="Fullerene">\n        fullerenes\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">\n        carbon nanotubes\n       </a>\n       ; though perhaps these examples should be included among the chemical elements, as they are composed entirely of\n       <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">\n        carbon\n       </a>\n       ).\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Elementary_properties_of_superconductors">\n        Elementary properties of superconductors\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Superconductivity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Elementary properties of superconductors">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       Most of the physical properties of superconductors vary from material to material, such as the\n       <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity" title="Heat capacity">\n        heat capacity\n       </a>\n       and the critical temperature, critical field, and critical current density at which superconductivity is destroyed.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       On the other hand, there is a class of properties that are independent of the underlying material. For instance, all superconductors have\n       <i>\n        exactly\n       </i>\n       zero resistivity to low applied currents when there is no magnetic field present or if the applied field does not exceed a critical value. The existence of these "universal" properties implies that superconductivity is a\n       <a href="/wiki/Phase_(matter)" title="Phase (matter)">\n        thermodynamic phase\n       </a>\n       , and thus possesses certain distinguishing properties which are largely independent of microscopic details.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Zero_electrical_DC_resistance">\n        Zero electrical DC resistance\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Superconductivity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Zero electrical DC resistance">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CERN-cables-p1030764.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/CERN-cables-p1030764.jpg/220px-CERN-cables-p1030764.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/CERN-cables-p1030764.jpg/330px-CERN-cables-p1030764.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/CERN-cables-p1030764.jpg/440px-CERN-cables-p1030764.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CERN-cables-p1030764.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Electric cables for accelerators at\n         <a href="/wiki/CERN" title="CERN">\n          CERN\n         </a>\n         . Both the massive and slim cables are rated for 12,500\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amperes" title="Amperes">\n          A\n         </a>\n         .\n         <i>\n          Top:\n         </i>\n         conventional cables for\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LEP" title="LEP">\n          LEP\n         </a>\n         ;\n         <i>\n          bottom:\n         </i>\n         superconductor-based cables for the\n         <a href="/wiki/Large_Hadron_Collider" title="Large Hadron Collider">\n          LHC\n         </a>\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cross_section_of_preform_superconductor_cable.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="584" data-file-width="778" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Cross_section_of_preform_superconductor_cable.jpg/220px-Cross_section_of_preform_superconductor_cable.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Cross_section_of_preform_superconductor_cable.jpg/330px-Cross_section_of_preform_superconductor_cable.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Cross_section_of_preform_superconductor_cable.jpg/440px-Cross_section_of_preform_superconductor_cable.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cross_section_of_preform_superconductor_cable.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Cross section of a preform superconductor rod from abandoned\n         <a href="/wiki/Superconducting_Super_Collider" title="Superconducting Super Collider">\n          Texas Superconducting Super Collider (SSC)\n         </a>\n         .\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The simplest method to measure the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_resistance" title="Electrical resistance">\n        electrical resistance\n       </a>\n       of a sample of some material is to place it in an\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_circuit" title="Electrical circuit">\n        electrical circuit\n       </a>\n       in series with a\n       <a href="/wiki/Current_source" title="Current source">\n        current source\n       </a>\n       <i>\n        I\n       </i>\n       and measure the resulting\n       <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">\n        voltage\n       </a>\n       <i>\n        V\n       </i>\n       across the sample. The resistance of the sample is given by\n       <a href="/wiki/Ohm%27s_law" title="Ohm\'s law">\n        Ohm\'s law\n       </a>\n       as\n       <i>\n        R\xc2\xa0=\xc2\xa0V\xc2\xa0/\xc2\xa0I\n       </i>\n       . If the voltage is zero, this means that the resistance is zero.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Superconductors are also able to maintain a current with no applied voltage whatsoever, a property exploited in\n       <a href="/wiki/Superconducting_magnet" title="Superconducting magnet">\n        superconducting electromagnets\n       </a>\n       such as those found in\n       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Magnetic resonance imaging">\n        MRI\n       </a>\n       machines. Experiments have demonstrated that currents in superconducting coils can persist for years without any measurable degradation. Experimental evidence points to a current lifetime of at least 100,000 years. Theoretical estimates for the lifetime of a persistent current can exceed the estimated lifetime of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">\n        universe\n       </a>\n       , depending on the wire geometry and the temperature.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gallop_3-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Gallop-3">\n         [3]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In practice, currents injected in superconducting coils have persisted for more than 22 years in superconducting\n       <a class="external text" href="http://www.astro.oma.be/en/world-record-in-the-underground-laboratory-of-membach/" rel="nofollow">\n        gravimeters\n       </a>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">\n        <a href="#cite_note-6">\n         [6]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       . In such instruments, the measurement principle is based on the monitoring of the levitation of a superconducting niobium sphere of mass 4 grams.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In a normal conductor, an electric current may be visualized as a fluid of\n       <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">\n        electrons\n       </a>\n       moving across a heavy\n       <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">\n        ionic\n       </a>\n       lattice. The electrons are constantly colliding with the ions in the lattice, and during each collision some of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">\n        energy\n       </a>\n       carried by the current is absorbed by the lattice and converted into\n       <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">\n        heat\n       </a>\n       , which is essentially the vibrational\n       <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">\n        kinetic energy\n       </a>\n       of the lattice ions. As a result, the energy carried by the current is constantly being dissipated. This is the phenomenon of electrical resistance and\n       <a href="/wiki/Joule_heating" title="Joule heating">\n        Joule heating\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The situation is different in a superconductor. In a conventional superconductor, the electronic fluid cannot be resolved into individual electrons. Instead, it consists of bound\n       <i>\n        pairs\n       </i>\n       of electrons known as\n       <a href="/wiki/Cooper_pair" title="Cooper pair">\n        Cooper pairs\n       </a>\n       . This pairing is caused by an attractive force between electrons from the exchange of\n       <a href="/wiki/Phonon" title="Phonon">\n        phonons\n       </a>\n       . Due to\n       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">\n        quantum mechanics\n       </a>\n       , the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_spectrum" title="Energy spectrum">\n        energy spectrum\n       </a>\n       of this Cooper pair fluid possesses an\n       <i>\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_gap" title="Energy gap">\n         energy gap\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       , meaning there is a minimum amount of energy \xce\x94\n       <i>\n        E\n       </i>\n       that must be supplied in order to excite the fluid. Therefore, if \xce\x94\n       <i>\n        E\n       </i>\n       is larger than the\n       <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">\n        thermal energy\n       </a>\n       of the lattice, given by\n       <i>\n        kT\n       </i>\n       , where\n       <i>\n        k\n       </i>\n       is\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boltzmann%27s_constant" title="Boltzmann\'s constant">\n        Boltzmann\'s constant\n       </a>\n       and\n       <i>\n        T\n       </i>\n       is the\n       <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">\n        temperature\n       </a>\n       , the fluid will not be scattered by the lattice. The Cooper pair fluid is thus a\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superfluid" title="Superfluid">\n        superfluid\n       </a>\n       , meaning it can flow without energy dissipation.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In a class of superconductors known as\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_II_superconductor" title="Type II superconductor">\n        type II superconductors\n       </a>\n       , including all known\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-temperature_superconductor" title="High-temperature superconductor">\n        high-temperature superconductors\n       </a>\n       , an extremely low but nonzero resistivity appears at temperatures not too far below the nominal superconducting transition when an electric current is applied in conjunction with a strong magnetic field, which may be caused by the electric current. This is due to the motion of\n       <a href="/wiki/Abrikosov_vortex" title="Abrikosov vortex">\n        magnetic vortices\n       </a>\n       in the electronic superfluid, which dissipates some of the energy carried by the current. If the current is sufficiently small, the vortices are stationary, and the resistivity vanishes. The resistance due to this effect is tiny compared with that of non-superconducting materials, but must be taken into account in sensitive experiments. However, as the temperature decreases far enough below the nominal superconducting transition, these vortices can become frozen into a disordered but stationary phase known as a "vortex glass". Below this vortex glass transition temperature, the resistance of the material becomes truly zero.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Superconducting_phase_transition">\n        Superconducting phase transition\n        <span id="Superconducting_phase_transition">\n        </span>\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Superconductivity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Superconducting phase transition">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cvandrhovst.png">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="483" data-file-width="681" height="284" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Cvandrhovst.png/400px-Cvandrhovst.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Cvandrhovst.png/600px-Cvandrhovst.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/08/Cvandrhovst.png 2x" width="400"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cvandrhovst.png" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Behavior of heat capacity (c\n         <sub>\n          v\n         </sub>\n         , blue) and resistivity (\xcf\x81, green) at the superconducting phase transition\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       In superconducting materials, the characteristics of superconductivity appear when the\n       <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">\n        temperature\n       </a>\n       <i>\n        T\n       </i>\n       is lowered below a critical temperature\n       <i>\n        T\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        c\n       </sub>\n       . The value of this critical temperature varies from material to material. Conventional superconductors usually have critical temperatures ranging from around 20\n       <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">\n        K\n       </a>\n       to less than 1\xc2\xa0K. Solid\n       <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">\n        mercury\n       </a>\n       , for example, has a critical temperature of 4.2\xc2\xa0K. As of 2009\n       <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">\n        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Superconductivity&amp;action=edit">\n         [update]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       , the highest critical temperature found for a conventional superconductor is 39 K for\n       <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_diboride" title="Magnesium diboride">\n        magnesium diboride\n       </a>\n       (MgB\n       <sub>\n        2\n       </sub>\n       ),\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">\n        <a href="#cite_note-7">\n         [7]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">\n        <a href="#cite_note-8">\n         [8]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       although this material displays enough exotic properties that there is some doubt about classifying it as a "conventional" superconductor.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">\n        <a href="#cite_note-9">\n         [9]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <a href="/wiki/Cuprate_superconductor" title="Cuprate superconductor">\n        Cuprate superconductors\n       </a>\n       can have much higher critical temperatures:\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/YBCO" title="YBCO">\n        YBa\n        <sub>\n         2\n        </sub>\n        Cu\n        <sub>\n         3\n        </sub>\n        O\n        <sub>\n         7\n        </sub>\n       </a>\n       , one of the first cuprate superconductors to be discovered, has a critical temperature of 92\xc2\xa0K, and mercury-based cuprates have been found with critical temperatures in excess of 130\xc2\xa0K. The explanation for these high critical temperatures remains unknown. Electron pairing due to\n       <a href="/wiki/Phonon" title="Phonon">\n        phonon\n       </a>\n       exchanges explains superconductivity in conventional superconductors, but it does not explain superconductivity in the newer superconductors that have a very high critical temperature.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Similarly, at a fixed temperature below the critical temperature, superconducting materials cease to superconduct when an external\n       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">\n        magnetic field\n       </a>\n       is applied which is greater than the\n       <i>\n        critical magnetic field\n       </i>\n       . This is because the\n       <a href="/wiki/Gibbs_free_energy" title="Gibbs free energy">\n        Gibbs free energy\n       </a>\n       of the superconducting phase increases quadratically with the magnetic field while the free energy of the normal phase is roughly independent of the magnetic field. If the material superconducts in the absence of a field, then the superconducting phase free energy is lower than that of the normal phase and so for some finite value of the magnetic field (proportional to the square root of the difference of the free energies at zero magnetic field) the two free energies will be equal and a phase transition to the normal phase will occur. More generally, a higher temperature and a stronger magnetic field lead to a smaller fraction of the electrons in the superconducting band and consequently a longer\n       <a href="/wiki/London_penetration_depth" title="London penetration depth">\n        London penetration depth\n       </a>\n       of external magnetic fields and currents. The penetration depth becomes infinite at the phase transition.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The onset of superconductivity is accompanied by abrupt changes in various physical properties, which is the hallmark of a\n       <a href="/wiki/Phase_transition" title="Phase transition">\n        phase transition\n       </a>\n       . For example, the electronic\n       <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity" title="Heat capacity">\n        heat capacity\n       </a>\n       is proportional to the temperature in the normal (non-superconducting) regime. At the superconducting transition, it suffers a discontinuous jump and thereafter ceases to be linear. At low temperatures, it varies instead as\n       <i>\n        e\n       </i>\n       <sup>\n        \xe2\x88\x92\xce\xb1/\n        <i>\n         T\n        </i>\n       </sup>\n       for some constant, \xce\xb1. This exponential behavior is one of the pieces of evidence for the existence of the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_gap" title="Energy gap">\n        energy gap\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second-order_transition" title="Second-order transition">\n        order\n       </a>\n       of the superconducting phase transition was long a matter of debate. Experiments indicate that the transition is second-order, meaning there is no\n       <a href="/wiki/Latent_heat" title="Latent heat">\n        latent heat\n       </a>\n       . However, in the presence of an external magnetic field there is latent heat, because the superconducting phase has a lower entropy below the critical temperature than the normal phase. It has been experimentally demonstrated\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">\n        <a href="#cite_note-10">\n         [10]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       that, as a consequence, when the magnetic field is increased beyond the critical field, the resulting phase transition leads to a decrease in the temperature of the superconducting material.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Calculations in the 1970s suggested that it may actually be weakly first-order due to the effect of long-range fluctuations in the electromagnetic field. In the 1980s it was shown theoretically with the help of a\n       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Disorder_field&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Disorder field (page does not exist)">\n        disorder field theory\n       </a>\n       , in which the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vortex_line" title="Vortex line">\n        vortex lines\n       </a>\n       of the superconductor play a major role, that the transition is of second order within the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_II_superconductor" title="Type II superconductor">\n        type II\n       </a>\n       regime and of first order (i.e.,\n       <a href="/wiki/Latent_heat" title="Latent heat">\n        latent heat\n       </a>\n       ) within the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_I_superconductor" title="Type I superconductor">\n        type I\n       </a>\n       regime, and that the two regions are separated by a\n       <a href="/wiki/Tricritical_point" title="Tricritical point">\n        tricritical point\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">\n        <a href="#cite_note-11">\n         [11]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The results were strongly supported by Monte Carlo computer simulations.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">\n        <a href="#cite_note-12">\n         [12]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Meissner_effect">\n        Meissner effect\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Superconductivity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Meissner effect">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Meissner_effect" title="Meissner effect">\n        Meissner effect\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       When a superconductor is placed in a weak external\n       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">\n        magnetic field\n       </a>\n       <b>\n        H\n       </b>\n       , and cooled below its transition temperature, the magnetic field is ejected. The Meissner effect does not cause the field to be completely ejected but instead the field penetrates the superconductor but only to a very small distance, characterized by a parameter\n       <i>\n        \xce\xbb\n       </i>\n       , called the\n       <a href="/wiki/London_penetration_depth" title="London penetration depth">\n        London penetration depth\n       </a>\n       , decaying exponentially to zero within the bulk of the material. The\n       <a href="/wiki/Meissner_effect" title="Meissner effect">\n        Meissner effect\n       </a>\n       is a defining characteristic of superconductivity. For most superconductors, the London penetration depth is on the order of 100\xc2\xa0nm.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The Meissner effect is sometimes confused with the kind of\n       <a href="/wiki/Diamagnetism" title="Diamagnetism">\n        diamagnetism\n       </a>\n       one would expect in a perfect electrical conductor: according to\n       <a href="/wiki/Lenz%27s_law" title="Lenz\'s law">\n        Lenz\'s law\n       </a>\n       , when a\n       <i>\n        changing\n       </i>\n       magnetic field is applied to a conductor, it will induce an electric current in the conductor that creates an opposing magnetic field. In a perfect conductor, an arbitrarily large current can be induced, and the resulting magnetic field exactly cancels the applied field.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The Meissner effect is distinct from this\xe2\x80\x94it is the spontaneous expulsion which occurs during transition to superconductivity. Suppose we have a material in its normal state, containing a constant internal magnetic field. When the material is cooled below the critical temperature, we would observe the abrupt expulsion of the internal magnetic field, which we would not expect based on Lenz\'s law.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The Meissner effect was given a phenomenological explanation by the brothers\n       <a href="/wiki/Fritz_London" title="Fritz London">\n        Fritz\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Heinz_London" title="Heinz London">\n        Heinz London\n       </a>\n       , who showed that the electromagnetic\n       <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_free_energy" title="Thermodynamic free energy">\n        free energy\n       </a>\n       in a superconductor is minimized provided\n      </p>\n      <dl>\n       <dd>\n        <span class="mwe-math-element">\n         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n          <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n           <semantics>\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n              <msup>\n               <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                \xe2\x88\x87\n                <!-- \xe2\x88\x87 -->\n               </mi>\n               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mn>\n                 2\n                </mn>\n               </mrow>\n              </msup>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mi mathvariant="bold">\n                H\n               </mi>\n              </mrow>\n              <mo>\n               =\n              </mo>\n              <msup>\n               <mi>\n                \xce\xbb\n                <!-- \xce\xbb -->\n               </mi>\n               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mo>\n                 \xe2\x88\x92\n                 <!-- \xe2\x88\x92 -->\n                </mo>\n                <mn>\n                 2\n                </mn>\n               </mrow>\n              </msup>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mi mathvariant="bold">\n                H\n               </mi>\n              </mrow>\n              <mspace width="thinmathspace">\n              </mspace>\n             </mstyle>\n            </mrow>\n            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\n             {\\displaystyle \\nabla ^{2}\\mathbf {H} =\\lambda ^{-2}\\mathbf {H} \\,}\n            </annotation>\n           </semantics>\n          </math>\n         </span>\n         <img alt="\\nabla ^{2}\\mathbf {H} =\\lambda ^{-2}\\mathbf {H} \\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/57d717daee621d38ce18f56caf7ddb65f191e567" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:14.421ex; height:2.676ex;"/>\n        </span>\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <p>\n       where\n       <b>\n        H\n       </b>\n       is the magnetic field and \xce\xbb is the\n       <a href="/wiki/London_penetration_depth" title="London penetration depth">\n        London penetration depth\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       This equation, which is known as the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/London_equation" title="London equation">\n        London equation\n       </a>\n       , predicts that the magnetic field in a superconductor\n       <a href="/wiki/Exponential_decay" title="Exponential decay">\n        decays exponentially\n       </a>\n       from whatever value it possesses at the surface.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       A superconductor with little or no magnetic field within it is said to be in the Meissner state. The Meissner state breaks down when the applied magnetic field is too large. Superconductors can be divided into two classes according to how this breakdown occurs. In\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_I_superconductor" title="Type I superconductor">\n        Type I superconductors\n       </a>\n       , superconductivity is abruptly destroyed when the strength of the applied field rises above a critical value\n       <i>\n        H\n        <sub>\n         c\n        </sub>\n       </i>\n       . Depending on the geometry of the sample, one may obtain an intermediate state\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">\n        <a href="#cite_note-13">\n         [13]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       consisting of a baroque pattern\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">\n        <a href="#cite_note-14">\n         [14]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       of regions of normal material carrying a magnetic field mixed with regions of superconducting material containing no field. In\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_II_superconductor" title="Type II superconductor">\n        Type II superconductors\n       </a>\n       , raising the applied field past a critical value\n       <i>\n        H\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        <i>\n         c\n        </i>\n        1\n       </sub>\n       leads to a mixed state (also known as the vortex state) in which an increasing amount of\n       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_flux" title="Magnetic flux">\n        magnetic flux\n       </a>\n       penetrates the material, but there remains no resistance to the flow of electric current as long as the current is not too large. At a second critical field strength\n       <i>\n        H\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        <i>\n         c\n        </i>\n        2\n       </sub>\n       , superconductivity is destroyed. The mixed state is actually caused by vortices in the electronic superfluid, sometimes called\n       <a href="/wiki/Fluxon" title="Fluxon">\n        fluxons\n       </a>\n       because the flux carried by these vortices is\n       <a href="/wiki/Quantum" title="Quantum">\n        quantized\n       </a>\n       . Most pure\n       <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">\n        elemental\n       </a>\n       superconductors, except\n       <a href="/wiki/Niobium" title="Niobium">\n        niobium\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">\n        carbon nanotubes\n       </a>\n       , are Type\xc2\xa0I, while almost all impure and compound superconductors are Type\xc2\xa0II.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="London_moment">\n        London moment\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Superconductivity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: London moment">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       Conversely, a spinning superconductor generates a magnetic field, precisely aligned with the spin axis. The effect, the\n       <a href="/wiki/London_moment" title="London moment">\n        London moment\n       </a>\n       , was put to good use in\n       <a href="/wiki/Gravity_Probe_B" title="Gravity Probe B">\n        Gravity Probe\xc2\xa0B\n       </a>\n       . This experiment measured the magnetic fields of four superconducting gyroscopes to determine their spin axes. This was critical to the experiment since it is one of the few ways to accurately determine the spin axis of an otherwise featureless sphere.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       <span id="Theories_of_superconductivity">\n       </span>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_superconductivity">\n        History of superconductivity\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Superconductivity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: History of superconductivity">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ehrenfest_Lorentz_Bohr_Kamerlingh_Onnes.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="348" data-file-width="500" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Ehrenfest_Lorentz_Bohr_Kamerlingh_Onnes.jpg/220px-Ehrenfest_Lorentz_Bohr_Kamerlingh_Onnes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Ehrenfest_Lorentz_Bohr_Kamerlingh_Onnes.jpg/330px-Ehrenfest_Lorentz_Bohr_Kamerlingh_Onnes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Ehrenfest_Lorentz_Bohr_Kamerlingh_Onnes.jpg/440px-Ehrenfest_Lorentz_Bohr_Kamerlingh_Onnes.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ehrenfest_Lorentz_Bohr_Kamerlingh_Onnes.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Heike Kamerlingh Onnes (right), the discoverer of superconductivity.\n         <a href="/wiki/Paul_Ehrenfest" title="Paul Ehrenfest">\n          Paul Ehrenfest\n         </a>\n         ,\n         <a href="/wiki/Hendrik_Lorentz" title="Hendrik Lorentz">\n          Hendrik Lorentz\n         </a>\n         ,\n         <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr" title="Niels Bohr">\n          Niels Bohr\n         </a>\n         stand to his left.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/History_of_superconductivity" title="History of superconductivity">\n        History of superconductivity\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Superconductivity was discovered on April 8, 1911 by\n       <a href="/wiki/Heike_Kamerlingh_Onnes" title="Heike Kamerlingh Onnes">\n        Heike Kamerlingh Onnes\n       </a>\n       , who was studying the resistance of solid\n       <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">\n        mercury\n       </a>\n       at\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryogenic" title="Cryogenic">\n        cryogenic\n       </a>\n       temperatures using the recently produced\n       <a href="/wiki/Liquid_helium" title="Liquid helium">\n        liquid helium\n       </a>\n       as a\n       <a href="/wiki/Refrigerant" title="Refrigerant">\n        refrigerant\n       </a>\n       . At the temperature of 4.2\xc2\xa0K, he observed that the resistance abruptly disappeared.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">\n        <a href="#cite_note-15">\n         [15]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In the same experiment, he also observed the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superfluid" title="Superfluid">\n        superfluid\n       </a>\n       transition of helium at 2.2\xc2\xa0K, without recognizing its significance. The precise date and circumstances of the discovery were only reconstructed a century later, when Onnes\'s notebook was found.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">\n        <a href="#cite_note-16">\n         [16]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In subsequent decades, superconductivity was observed in several other materials. In 1913,\n       <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">\n        lead\n       </a>\n       was found to superconduct at 7\xc2\xa0K, and in 1941\n       <a href="/wiki/Niobium_nitride" title="Niobium nitride">\n        niobium nitride\n       </a>\n       was found to superconduct at 16\xc2\xa0K.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Great efforts have been devoted to finding out how and why superconductivity works; the important step occurred in 1933, when\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walter_Meissner" title="Walter Meissner">\n        Meissner\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Robert_Ochsenfeld" title="Robert Ochsenfeld">\n        Ochsenfeld\n       </a>\n       discovered that superconductors expelled applied magnetic fields, a phenomenon which has come to be known as the\n       <a href="/wiki/Meissner_effect" title="Meissner effect">\n        Meissner effect\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">\n        <a href="#cite_note-17">\n         [17]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In 1935,\n       <a href="/wiki/Fritz_London" title="Fritz London">\n        Fritz\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Heinz_London" title="Heinz London">\n        Heinz London\n       </a>\n       showed that the Meissner effect was a consequence of the minimization of the electromagnetic\n       <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_free_energy" title="Thermodynamic free energy">\n        free energy\n       </a>\n       carried by superconducting current.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">\n        <a href="#cite_note-18">\n         [18]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="London_theory">\n        London theory\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Superconductivity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: London theory">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       The first phenomenological theory of superconductivity was\n       <a href="/wiki/London_equations" title="London equations">\n        London theory\n       </a>\n       . It was put forward by the brothers Fritz and Heinz London in 1935, shortly after the discovery that magnetic fields are expelled from superconductors. A major triumph of the equations of this theory is their ability to explain the\n       <a href="/wiki/Meissner_effect" title="Meissner effect">\n        Meissner effect\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">\n        <a href="#cite_note-19">\n         [19]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       wherein a material exponentially expels all internal magnetic fields as it crosses the superconducting threshold. By using the London equation, one can obtain the dependence of the magnetic field inside the superconductor on the distance to the surface.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">\n        <a href="#cite_note-20">\n         [20]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       There are two London equations:\n      </p>\n      <dl>\n       <dd>\n        <span class="mwe-math-element">\n         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n          <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n      </span>\n         <img alt="{\\frac {\\partial \\mathbf {j} _{s}}{\\partial t}}={\\frac {n_{s}e^{2}}{m}}\\mathbf {E} ,\\qquad \\mathbf {\\nabla } \\times \\mathbf {j} _{s}=-{\\frac
