style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:39.514ex; height:5.843ex;"/>\n        </span>\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <p>\n       The first equation follows from\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_second_law" title="Newton\'s second law">\n        Newton\'s second law\n       </a>\n       for superconducting electrons.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span id="Conventional_theories_(1950s)">\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Conventional_theories_.281950s.29">\n        Conventional theories (1950s)\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Superconductivity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Conventional theories (1950s)">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       During the 1950s, theoretical\n       <a href="/wiki/Condensed_matter_physics" title="Condensed matter physics">\n        condensed matter\n       </a>\n       physicists arrived at an understanding of "conventional" superconductivity, through a pair of remarkable and important theories: the phenomenological\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ginzburg-Landau_theory" title="Ginzburg-Landau theory">\n        Ginzburg-Landau theory\n       </a>\n       (1950) and the microscopic\n       <a href="/wiki/BCS_theory" title="BCS theory">\n        BCS theory\n       </a>\n       (1957).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">\n        <a href="#cite_note-21">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BardeenCooperSchrieffer_22-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-BardeenCooperSchrieffer-22">\n         [22]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In 1950, the\n       <a href="/wiki/Phenomenology_(particle_physics)" title="Phenomenology (particle physics)">\n        phenomenological\n       </a>\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ginzburg-Landau_theory" title="Ginzburg-Landau theory">\n        Ginzburg-Landau theory\n       </a>\n       of superconductivity was devised by\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lev_Davidovich_Landau" title="Lev Davidovich Landau">\n        Landau\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vitalij_Lazarevics_Ginzburg" title="Vitalij Lazarevics Ginzburg">\n        Ginzburg\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">\n        <a href="#cite_note-23">\n         [23]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This theory, which combined Landau\'s theory of second-order\n       <a href="/wiki/Phase_transition" title="Phase transition">\n        phase transitions\n       </a>\n       with a\n       <a href="/wiki/Schr%C3%B6dinger_equation" title="Schr\xc3\xb6dinger equation">\n        Schr\xc3\xb6dinger\n       </a>\n       -like wave equation, had great success in explaining the macroscopic properties of superconductors. In particular,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexei_Alexeevich_Abrikosov" title="Alexei Alexeevich Abrikosov">\n        Abrikosov\n       </a>\n       showed that Ginzburg-Landau theory predicts the division of superconductors into the two categories now referred to as Type\xc2\xa0I and Type\xc2\xa0II. Abrikosov and Ginzburg were awarded the 2003 Nobel Prize for their work (Landau had received the 1962 Nobel Prize for other work, and died in 1968). The four-dimensional extension of the Ginzburg-Landau theory, the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coleman-Weinberg_potential" title="Coleman-Weinberg potential">\n        Coleman-Weinberg model\n       </a>\n       , is important in\n       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">\n        quantum field theory\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">\n        cosmology\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Also in 1950, Maxwell and Reynolds\n       <i>\n        et al.\n       </i>\n       found that the critical temperature of a superconductor depends on the\n       <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">\n        isotopic mass\n       </a>\n       of the constituent\n       <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">\n        element\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">\n        <a href="#cite_note-24">\n         [24]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">\n        <a href="#cite_note-25">\n         [25]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This important discovery pointed to the\n       <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">\n        electron\n       </a>\n       -\n       <a href="/wiki/Phonon" title="Phonon">\n        phonon\n       </a>\n       interaction as the microscopic mechanism responsible for superconductivity.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The complete microscopic theory of superconductivity was finally proposed in 1957 by\n       <a href="/wiki/John_Bardeen" title="John Bardeen">\n        Bardeen\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leon_Neil_Cooper" title="Leon Neil Cooper">\n        Cooper\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/John_Robert_Schrieffer" title="John Robert Schrieffer">\n        Schrieffer\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BardeenCooperSchrieffer_22-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-BardeenCooperSchrieffer-22">\n         [22]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This BCS theory explained the superconducting current as a\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superfluid" title="Superfluid">\n        superfluid\n       </a>\n       of\n       <a href="/wiki/Cooper_pair" title="Cooper pair">\n        Cooper pairs\n       </a>\n       , pairs of electrons interacting through the exchange of phonons. For this work, the authors were awarded the Nobel Prize in 1972.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The BCS theory was set on a firmer footing in 1958, when\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N._N._Bogolyubov" title="N. N. Bogolyubov">\n        N. N. Bogolyubov\n       </a>\n       showed that the BCS wavefunction, which had originally been derived from a variational argument, could be obtained using a canonical transformation of the electronic\n       <a href="/wiki/Hamiltonian_(quantum_mechanics)" title="Hamiltonian (quantum mechanics)">\n        Hamiltonian\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">\n        <a href="#cite_note-26">\n         [26]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In 1959,\n       <a href="/wiki/Lev_Gor%27kov" title="Lev Gor\'kov">\n        Lev Gor\'kov\n       </a>\n       showed that the BCS theory reduced to the Ginzburg-Landau theory close to the critical temperature.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">\n        <a href="#cite_note-27">\n         [27]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BCS-boboliubov_28-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-BCS-boboliubov-28">\n         [28]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Generalizations of BCS theory for conventional superconductors form the basis for understanding of the phenomenon of\n       <a href="/wiki/Superfluidity" title="Superfluidity">\n        superfluidity\n       </a>\n       , because they fall into the\n       <a href="/wiki/Lambda_transition" title="Lambda transition">\n        lambda transition\n       </a>\n       universality class. The extent to which such generalizations can be applied to\n       <a href="/wiki/Unconventional_superconductor" title="Unconventional superconductor">\n        unconventional superconductors\n       </a>\n       is still controversial.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Further_history">\n        Further history\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Superconductivity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Further history">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       The first practical application of superconductivity was developed in 1954 with\n       <a href="/wiki/Dudley_Allen_Buck" title="Dudley Allen Buck">\n        Dudley Allen Buck\n       </a>\n       \'s invention of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Cryotron" title="Cryotron">\n        cryotron\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mit-memo_29-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-mit-memo-29">\n         [29]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Two superconductors with greatly different values of critical magnetic field are combined to produce a fast, simple switch for computer elements.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Soon after discovering superconductivity in 1911, Kamerlingh Onnes attempted to make an electromagnet with superconducting windings but found that relatively low magnetic fields destroyed superconductivity in the materials he investigated. Much later, in 1955, G.B. Yntema\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">\n        <a href="#cite_note-30">\n         [30]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       succeeded in constructing a small 0.7-tesla iron-core electromagnet with superconducting niobium wire windings. Then, in 1961, J.E. Kunzler, E. Buehler, F.S.L. Hsu, and J.H. Wernick\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">\n        <a href="#cite_note-31">\n         [31]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       made the startling discovery that, at 4.2 kelvin, a compound consisting of three parts niobium and one part tin, was capable of supporting a current density of more than 100,000 amperes per square centimeter in a magnetic field of 8.8 tesla. Despite being brittle and difficult to fabricate, niobium-tin has since proved extremely useful in supermagnets generating magnetic fields as high as 20 tesla. In 1962 T.G. Berlincourt and R.R. Hake\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">\n        <a href="#cite_note-32">\n         [32]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">\n        <a href="#cite_note-33">\n         [33]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       discovered that alloys of niobium and titanium are suitable for applications up to 10 tesla. Promptly thereafter, commercial production of niobium-titanium supermagnet wire commenced at Westinghouse Electric Corporation and at Wah Chang Corporation. Although niobium-titanium boasts less-impressive superconducting properties than those of niobium-tin, niobium-titanium has, nevertheless, become the most widely used \xe2\x80\x9cworkhorse\xe2\x80\x9d supermagnet material, in large measure a consequence of its very-high ductility and ease of fabrication. However, both niobium-tin and niobium-titanium find wide application in MRI medical imagers, bending and focusing magnets for enormous high-energy-particle accelerators, and a host of other applications. Conectus, a European superconductivity consortium, estimated that in 2014, global economic activity for which superconductivity was indispensable amounted to about five billion euros, with MRI systems accounting for about 80% of that total.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In 1962,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brian_David_Josephson" title="Brian David Josephson">\n        Josephson\n       </a>\n       made the important theoretical prediction that a supercurrent can flow between two pieces of superconductor separated by a thin layer of insulator.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">\n        <a href="#cite_note-34">\n         [34]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This phenomenon, now called the\n       <a href="/wiki/Josephson_effect" title="Josephson effect">\n        Josephson effect\n       </a>\n       , is exploited by superconducting devices such as\n       <a href="/wiki/SQUID" title="SQUID">\n        SQUIDs\n       </a>\n       . It is used in the most accurate available measurements of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_flux_quantum" title="Magnetic flux quantum">\n        magnetic flux quantum\n       </a>\n       <i>\n        \xce\xa6\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        0\n       </sub>\n       =\n       <i>\n        h\n       </i>\n       /(2\n       <i>\n        e\n       </i>\n       ), where\n       <i>\n        h\n       </i>\n       is the\n       <a href="/wiki/Planck_constant" title="Planck constant">\n        Planck constant\n       </a>\n       . Coupled with the\n       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_Hall_effect" title="Quantum Hall effect">\n        quantum Hall resistivity\n       </a>\n       , this leads to a precise measurement of the Planck constant. Josephson was awarded the Nobel Prize for this work in 1973.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In 2008, it was proposed that the same mechanism that produces superconductivity could produce a\n       <a href="/wiki/Superinsulator" title="Superinsulator">\n        superinsulator\n       </a>\n       state in some materials, with almost infinite\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_resistance" title="Electrical resistance">\n        electrical resistance\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">\n        <a href="#cite_note-35">\n         [35]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="High-temperature_superconductivity">\n        High-temperature superconductivity\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Superconductivity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: High-temperature superconductivity">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:480px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Timeline_of_Superconductivity_from_1900_to_2015.svg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="271" data-file-width="478" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Timeline_of_Superconductivity_from_1900_to_2015.svg/478px-Timeline_of_Superconductivity_from_1900_to_2015.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Timeline_of_Superconductivity_from_1900_to_2015.svg/717px-Timeline_of_Superconductivity_from_1900_to_2015.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Timeline_of_Superconductivity_from_1900_to_2015.svg/956px-Timeline_of_Superconductivity_from_1900_to_2015.svg.png 2x" width="478"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Timeline_of_Superconductivity_from_1900_to_2015.svg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Timeline of superconducting materials\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/High-temperature_superconductivity" title="High-temperature superconductivity">\n        High-temperature superconductivity\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Until 1986, physicists had believed that BCS theory forbade superconductivity at temperatures above about 30\xc2\xa0K. In that year,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johannes_Georg_Bednorz" title="Johannes Georg Bednorz">\n        Bednorz\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/K._Alex_M%C3%BCller" title="K. Alex M\xc3\xbcller">\n        M\xc3\xbcller\n       </a>\n       discovered superconductivity in a\n       <a href="/wiki/Lanthanum" title="Lanthanum">\n        lanthanum\n       </a>\n       -based cuprate\n       <a href="/wiki/Perovskite_(structure)" title="Perovskite (structure)">\n        perovskite\n       </a>\n       material, which had a transition temperature of 35\xc2\xa0K (Nobel Prize in Physics, 1987).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bednorz_5-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Bednorz-5">\n         [5]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       It was soon found that replacing the lanthanum with\n       <a href="/wiki/Yttrium" title="Yttrium">\n        yttrium\n       </a>\n       (i.e.,\xc2\xa0making\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/YBCO" title="YBCO">\n        YBCO\n       </a>\n       ) raised the critical temperature to 92\xc2\xa0K.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wu_36-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-wu-36">\n         [36]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       This temperature jump is particularly significant, since it allows\n       <a href="/wiki/Liquid_nitrogen" title="Liquid nitrogen">\n        liquid nitrogen\n       </a>\n       as a refrigerant, replacing\n       <a href="/wiki/Liquid_helium" title="Liquid helium">\n        liquid helium\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wu_36-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-wu-36">\n         [36]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This can be important commercially because liquid nitrogen can be produced relatively cheaply, even on-site. Also, the higher temperatures help avoid some of the problems that arise at liquid helium temperatures, such as the formation of plugs of frozen air that can block cryogenic lines and cause unanticipated and potentially hazardous pressure buildup.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">\n        <a href="#cite_note-37">\n         [37]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">\n        <a href="#cite_note-38">\n         [38]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Many other cuprate superconductors have since been discovered, and the theory of superconductivity in these materials is one of the major outstanding challenges of theoretical\n       <a href="/wiki/Condensed_matter_physics" title="Condensed matter physics">\n        condensed matter physics\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">\n        <a href="#cite_note-39">\n         [39]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       There are currently two main hypotheses \xe2\x80\x93 the\n       <a href="/wiki/Resonating_valence_bond_theory" title="Resonating valence bond theory">\n        resonating-valence-bond theory\n       </a>\n       , and spin fluctuation which has the most support in the research community.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">\n        <a href="#cite_note-40">\n         [40]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The second hypothesis proposed that electron pairing in high-temperature superconductors is mediated by short-range spin waves known as\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paramagnon" title="Paramagnon">\n        paramagnons\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">\n        <a href="#cite_note-41">\n         [41]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">\n        <a href="#cite_note-42">\n         [42]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        [\n        <i>\n         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute">\n          <span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (August 2016)">\n           dubious\n          </span>\n         </a>\n         <span class="metadata">\n          \xe2\x80\x93\n          <a href="/wiki/Talk:Superconductivity#Dubious" title="Talk:Superconductivity">\n           discuss\n          </a>\n         </span>\n        </i>\n        ]\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Since about 1993, the highest-temperature superconductor has been a ceramic material consisting of mercury, barium, calcium, copper and oxygen (HgBa\n       <sub>\n        2\n       </sub>\n       Ca\n       <sub>\n        2\n       </sub>\n       Cu\n       <sub>\n        3\n       </sub>\n       O\n       <sub>\n        8+\xce\xb4\n       </sub>\n       ) with\n       <i>\n        T\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        c\n       </sub>\n       = 133\xe2\x80\x93138 K.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aschi_43-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-aschi-43">\n         [43]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">\n        <a href="#cite_note-44">\n         [44]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The latter experiment (138 K) still awaits experimental confirmation, however.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In February 2008, an iron-based family of high-temperature superconductors was discovered.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">\n        <a href="#cite_note-45">\n         [45]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">\n        <a href="#cite_note-46">\n         [46]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Hideo Hosono, of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Institute_of_Technology" title="Tokyo Institute of Technology">\n        Tokyo Institute of Technology\n       </a>\n       , and colleagues found lanthanum oxygen fluorine iron arsenide (LaO\n       <sub>\n        1\xe2\x88\x92x\n       </sub>\n       F\n       <sub>\n        x\n       </sub>\n       FeAs), an\n       <a href="/wiki/Oxypnictide" title="Oxypnictide">\n        oxypnictide\n       </a>\n       that superconducts below 26\xc2\xa0K. Replacing the lanthanum in LaO\n       <sub>\n        1\xe2\x88\x92\n        <i>\n         x\n        </i>\n       </sub>\n       F\n       <sub>\n        <i>\n         x\n        </i>\n       </sub>\n       FeAs with\n       <a href="/wiki/Samarium" title="Samarium">\n        samarium\n       </a>\n       leads to superconductors that work at 55\xc2\xa0K.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">\n        <a href="#cite_note-47">\n         [47]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In May 2014,\n       <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">\n        hydrogen sulfide\n       </a>\n       (\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        H\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         <br/>\n         2\n        </span>\n        S\n       </span>\n       ) was predicted to be a high-temperature superconductor with a transition temperature of 80 K at 160 gigapascals of pressure.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">\n        <a href="#cite_note-48">\n         [48]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In 2015,\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        H\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         <br/>\n         2\n        </span>\n        S\n       </span>\n       has been observed to exhibit superconductivity at below 203\xc2\xa0K but at extremely high pressures \xe2\x80\x94 around 150 gigapascals.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DrozdovEremets2015_49-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-DrozdovEremets2015-49">\n         [49]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">\n        Applications\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Superconductivity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Applications">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Technological_applications_of_superconductivity" title="Technological applications of superconductivity">\n        Technological applications of superconductivity\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_2" style="width:220px;" videopayload=\'&lt;div data-framerate="29.97002997003"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;\'>\n         <img alt="File:Flyingsuperconductor.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Flyingsuperconductor.ogv/220px--Flyingsuperconductor.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:155px"/>\n         <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/08/Flyingsuperconductor.ogv" target="new" title="Play media">\n          <span class="play-btn-large">\n           <span class="mw-tmh-playtext">\n            Play media\n           </span>\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flyingsuperconductor.ogv" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Video of superconducting levitation of\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/YBCO" title="YBCO">\n          YBCO\n         </a>\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       <a href="/wiki/Superconducting_magnet" title="Superconducting magnet">\n        Superconducting magnets\n       </a>\n       are some of the most powerful\n       <a href="/wiki/Electromagnet" title="Electromagnet">\n        electromagnets\n       </a>\n       known. They are used in\n       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Magnetic resonance imaging">\n        MRI\n       </a>\n       /\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NMR" title="NMR">\n        NMR\n       </a>\n       machines,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometer" title="Mass spectrometer">\n        mass spectrometers\n       </a>\n       , the beam-steering magnets used in\n       <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">\n        particle accelerators\n       </a>\n       and plasma confining magnets in some\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tokamaks" title="Tokamaks">\n        tokamaks\n       </a>\n       . They can also be used for magnetic separation, where weakly magnetic particles are extracted from a background of less or non-magnetic particles, as in the\n       <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">\n        pigment\n       </a>\n       industries.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In the 1950s and 1960s, superconductors were used to build experimental digital computers using\n       <a href="/wiki/Cryotron" title="Cryotron">\n        cryotron\n       </a>\n       switches. More recently, superconductors have been used to make\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_circuit" title="Digital circuit">\n        digital circuits\n       </a>\n       based on\n       <a href="/wiki/Rapid_single_flux_quantum" title="Rapid single flux quantum">\n        rapid single flux quantum\n       </a>\n       technology and\n       <a href="/wiki/RF_and_microwave_filter" title="RF and microwave filter">\n        RF and microwave filters\n       </a>\n       for\n       <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone" title="Mobile phone">\n        mobile phone\n       </a>\n       base stations.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Superconductors are used to build\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Josephson_junction" title="Josephson junction">\n        Josephson junctions\n       </a>\n       which are the building blocks of\n       <a href="/wiki/SQUID" title="SQUID">\n        SQUIDs\n       </a>\n       (superconducting quantum interference devices), the most sensitive\n       <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">\n        magnetometers\n       </a>\n       known. SQUIDs are used in\n       <a href="/wiki/Scanning_SQUID_microscope" title="Scanning SQUID microscope">\n        scanning SQUID microscopes\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Magnetoencephalography" title="Magnetoencephalography">\n        magnetoencephalography\n       </a>\n       . Series of Josephson devices are used to realize the\n       <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">\n        SI\n       </a>\n       <a href="/wiki/Volt" title="Volt">\n        volt\n       </a>\n       . Depending on the particular mode of operation, a\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superconductor-insulator-superconductor" title="Superconductor-insulator-superconductor">\n        superconductor-insulator-superconductor\n       </a>\n       Josephson junction can be used as a photon\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detector" title="Detector">\n        detector\n       </a>\n       or as a\n       <a href="/wiki/Electronic_mixer" title="Electronic mixer">\n        mixer\n       </a>\n       . The large resistance change at the transition from the normal- to the superconducting state is used to build thermometers in cryogenic\n       <a href="/wiki/Calorimeter" title="Calorimeter">\n        micro-calorimeter\n       </a>\n       photon\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detector" title="Detector">\n        detectors\n       </a>\n       . The same effect is used in ultrasensitive\n       <a href="/wiki/Bolometer" title="Bolometer">\n        bolometers\n       </a>\n       made from superconducting materials.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Other early markets are arising where the relative efficiency, size and weight advantages of devices based on\n       <a href="/wiki/High-temperature_superconductivity" title="High-temperature superconductivity">\n        high-temperature superconductivity\n       </a>\n       outweigh the additional costs involved. For example, in\n       <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">\n        wind turbines\n       </a>\n       the lower weight and volume of superconducting generators could lead to savings in construction and tower costs, offsetting the higher costs for the generator and lowering the total LCOE.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">\n        <a href="#cite_note-50">\n         [50]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Promising future applications include high-performance\n       <a href="/wiki/Smart_grid" title="Smart grid">\n        smart grid\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_transmission" title="Electric power transmission">\n        electric power transmission\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Transformer" title="Transformer">\n        transformers\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SMES" title="SMES">\n        power storage devices\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">\n        electric motors\n       </a>\n       (e.g. for vehicle propulsion, as in\n       <a href="/wiki/Vactrain" title="Vactrain">\n        vactrains\n       </a>\n       or\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maglev_train" title="Maglev train">\n        maglev trains\n       </a>\n       ),\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_levitation_device" title="Magnetic levitation device">\n        magnetic levitation devices\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Fault_current_limiter" title="Fault current limiter">\n        fault current limiters\n       </a>\n       , enhancing spintronic devices with superconducting materials,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">\n        <a href="#cite_note-51">\n         [51]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and superconducting\n       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_refrigeration" title="Magnetic refrigeration">\n        magnetic refrigeration\n       </a>\n       . However, superconductivity is sensitive to moving magnetic fields so applications that use\n       <a href="/wiki/Alternating_current" title="Alternating current">\n        alternating current\n       </a>\n       (e.g. transformers) will be more difficult to develop than those that rely upon\n       <a href="/wiki/Direct_current" title="Direct current">\n        direct current\n       </a>\n       . Compared to traditional power lines superconducting transmission lines are more efficient and require only a fraction of the space, which would not only lead to a better environmental performance but could also improve public acceptance for expansion of the electric grid.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">\n        <a href="#cite_note-52">\n         [52]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Nobel_Prizes_for_superconductivity">\n        Nobel Prizes for superconductivity\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Superconductivity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Nobel Prizes for superconductivity">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        <a href="/wiki/Heike_Kamerlingh_Onnes" title="Heike Kamerlingh Onnes">\n         Heike Kamerlingh Onnes\n        </a>\n        (1913), "for his investigations on the properties of matter at low temperatures which led, inter alia, to the production of liquid helium"\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <a href="/wiki/John_Bardeen" title="John Bardeen">\n         John Bardeen\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leon_N._Cooper" title="Leon N. Cooper">\n         Leon N. Cooper\n        </a>\n        , and\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J._Robert_Schrieffer" title="J. Robert Schrieffer">\n         J. Robert Schrieffer\n        </a>\n        (1972), "for their jointly developed theory of superconductivity, usually called the BCS-theory"\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <a href="/wiki/Leo_Esaki" title="Leo Esaki">\n         Leo Esaki\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Ivar_Giaever" title="Ivar Giaever">\n         Ivar Giaever\n        </a>\n        , and\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brian_D._Josephson" title="Brian D. Josephson">\n         Brian D. Josephson\n        </a>\n        (1973), "for their experimental discoveries regarding tunneling phenomena in semiconductors and superconductors, respectively," and "for his theoretical predictions of the properties of a supercurrent through a tunnel barrier, in particular those phenomena which are generally known as the Josephson effects"\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <a href="/wiki/Georg_Bednorz" title="Georg Bednorz">\n         Georg Bednorz\n        </a>\n        and\n        <a href="/wiki/K._Alex_M%C3%BCller" title="K. Alex M\xc3\xbcller">\n         K. Alex M\xc3\xbcller\n        </a>\n        (1987), "for their important break-through in the discovery of superconductivity in ceramic materials"\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexei_A._Abrikosov" title="Alexei A. Abrikosov">\n         Alexei A. Abrikosov\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vitaly_L._Ginzburg" title="Vitaly L. Ginzburg">\n         Vitaly L. Ginzburg\n        </a>\n        , and\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthony_J._Leggett" title="Anthony J. Leggett">\n         Anthony J. Leggett\n        </a>\n        (2003), "for pioneering contributions to the theory of superconductors and superfluids"\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nobel_Prizes_in_Physics_53-0">\n         <a href="#cite_note-Nobel_Prizes_in_Physics-53">\n          [53]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michael_Kosterlitz" title="Michael Kosterlitz">\n         Michael Kosterlitz\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Duncan_Haldane" title="Duncan Haldane">\n         Duncan Haldane\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/David_J._Thouless" title="David J. Thouless">\n         David J. Thouless\n        </a>\n        (2016)\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">\n        See also\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n
